B00C061I3K|5.0|In retrospect this album was destined to go in a new direction. That's how Daft Punk rolls.<br /><br />But only now that I've heard it multiple times, can I truly appreciate that they are trying to plant a stake in the heart of the plateau that EDM and all its splinters have reached. Somehow Daft Punk takes any willing listener to a place that is wholly unexpected; a place that is simultaneously disco retro and truly utterly, zeitgeist-level now. I don't know how they can get such breezy, sweet, funky smoothness out of their tools but they've done it.<br /><br />Now I'll agree that upon first listen your ear is not going to be ready (mine wasn't) and if you only give the album a single listen-through you'll be truly missing out. But after the second listen, you'll pickup two or three more tracks ... then on the third listen the album just clicks and it will \\"click on the 24 track\\". Once you realize that this track is it... the ourobouros... both the beginning and end of the meta-loop of dance music from 1970's Donna Summer to music just before this album came out - then you can head in the new direction. I know this is high-falootin stuff and a rarified comparison, but if you've ever lost yourself to dance I'll bet you'll agree that this album is effectively the Dark Side of the Moon of Dance music.<br /><br />Quick track breakdown:<br />Stand out tracks: Give Life Back to Music, Giorgio by Moroder, Lose Yourself to Dance, Get Lucky, Doin' It Right, Contact<br />Just plain interesting Daft Punk-isms: Touch, Within, Fragments of Time.<br /><br />The final beauty of this whole thing is, if you recall every other album from Daft Punk, _this is just the beginning_. These tracks will find obvious, innocuous, and always compelling ways to be a part of other genres. Then that cross-pollination with the other genres will echo back into these song's remixes and ultimately spread more and more seeds for the continued birth of a new direction for dance.<br /><br />Give this album multiple open listens and you'll see those seeds now and how, by the end of this album, the world of dance music should not, will not, and can not be the same.

B00BXHLM1K|2.0|The 2013 RIGOLETTO, set in the Vegas of the Rat Pack era, was the opera debut of another in Met GM Peter Gelb's long line of distinguished non-opera directors. Once in a while there is a bit of stage business devised by Michael Mayer that suggests someone who does good work in his own milieu, for example, the hitman casually jotting his name on a matchbook while singing, and insistently pressing it into Rig's hand. But for all the ink spilled on the production's outrageousness (by those who hated it) and its innovations (mostly by those trying to sell tickets to it), this is essentially a cosmetic update. It puts a lot of bright color and \\"novelty\\" in front of us, and there is an air of self-satisfaction about it just for that. But it brings no special insight or point of view to the work, as real Regietheater is supposed to do. It is a busy traditional RIGOLETTO with different kinds of detritus in everyone's way.<br /><br />No one sounds particularly good (is this set an acoustic challenge, perhaps? Or was it just February cold season in New York?), and conductor Michele Mariotti is a klutz, making no better an impression with a great orchestra than he has in the past with a poor one (PURITANI from Bologna). His fast tempi are very fast, which is fine, but he simply lets the music tumble out shapelessly, leaving the singers to snatch breaths where they can. The result is almost anti-phrasing, and no matter how they are caricatured by their harshest critics, Toscanini and Muti did not lead performances of Italian opera this way. I was wincing even in the rapid-fire exchanges before \\"Questa o quella,\\" and matters did not improve. I doubt even native Italian speakers could make much of the words with such graceless leadership; this international cast is at sea.<br /><br />Zeljko Lucic (Rigoletto) and Diana Damrau (Gilda) have appeared previously in these roles in a 2008 DVD performance from Dresden conducted by Fabio Luisi, the production (also in modern dress) by Nikolaus Lehnhoff. Their Duke was Juan Diego Flrez. That performance is urgently recommended above this one. Lucic's blustery, unfocused singing at the Met is a liability. In his present state, he has the technical shortcomings of Tito Gobbi in this music (a constricted, hooded top and a flinty basic sound), but without the stylistic and expressive resources. Piotr Beczala has never looked better than in the Duke's white tux, but he has sung better, even at the Met (LUCIA, MANON). Stefan Kocan also looks great in the clothes, but his ubiquity in bass roles at the Met is hard to understand. The sound is lightweight for much of what is thrown his way, and he is not a special actor or interpreter, just a good-looking man with a pleasant timbre. For its previous video release of RIGOLETTO (1977), the Met could field Isola Jones for Maddalena and John Cheek for Monterone. Their 2013 counterparts are adequate and poor, respectively.<br /><br />Damrau merits slightly more extended discussion. Having recently given birth, she is heavier than usual and is undermined by costumes that are not as concealing as they could be. She is slow to warm up vocally, and does not sound close to a representative form until the opera's second half (the first father/daughter duet and \\"Caro nome\\" go for little...not the case in Dresden '08). But one thing must be said on her behalf: she understands this character. She gets things right that I believe owe less to Michael Mayer's direction than to experience and sympathy with the role of Gilda. There is a tragic ambiguity to Gilda's view of the Duke, and even when she realizes he is a cad, she carries feelings for him that she understands as love. She has given something of herself to him, and it is a payment the truth cannot refund to her. In the hands of someone who acts the part sensitively, Gilda is a very touching character, not an annoying or insipid one. On that part of the assignment, Damrau delivers.<br /><br />Another of Mayer's bits that I liked was Gilda's smelling the Duke's jacket while Rigoletto rages after his daughter's attack. This is eloquent and tasteful, and there are other such little moments in the margins, such as the direction of Marullo (ashamed) in the pleading episode of Rigoletto's big scene with the courtiers. Peter Gelb has been loyal to directors (giving Bartlett Sher and Mary Zimmerman chance after chance, despite their misfires); perhaps Mayer will be more on target the second time. More subtlety, less gimmickry, please.<br /><br />In truth, the best vocal performance is Met mezzo stalwart Maria Zifchak's deft Giovanna (in beehive). With all considerable due respect to Ms. Zifchak, this is not good news.<br /><br />The English subtitles are irritatingly updated to a '60s colloquial style of speaking (\\"doll\\" and \\"big shot\\" and \\"rubbed out\\" and so on). They frequently have only a handshake acquaintance with the text, and they are tin-eared overkill.<br /><br />While not a disaster, this was a \\"C\\"-grade afternoon for the Met, neither the best work of anyone involved nor an HD broadcast I will want to revisit. For all the bright lights on the stage, there is precious little in the way of illumination.

B009KY5OGC|5.0|This book is an eye opener. For those of us raised on patriotic stories of well meaning white people and dishonest Indians, this book will change everything. Over the years we have come to understand that our previous views of American Indians were wrong, but this lists those wrongs and more importantly it remembers great native Americans whose names should be remembered. It becomes apparent from the start of this book that the American Indians merely wanted their land...or even a small part of it. That they tried to comply and took far more abuse and dishonest dealings than any white American would have taken. This book covers each tragic tale and you see the repeated plea of great and failing Indian chiefs who are trying to save their people from annihilation. I love a non-fiction book where I can find new heroes to read about. This book provides a plethora of heroes, who were instead regarded as criminals by the US. 0374531269|4.0|\\"A Long Way Gone: Memoirs of a Boy Soldier\\" is an autobiography of Ishmael Beah's harrowing tale as a child soldier in Sierra Leone as a teenage boy. This book transports the reader to a part of Africa that is seemingly modern, yet tainted with war. This is just one account out of thousands of children that are kidnapped and forced to fight for a cause that isn't even logical. Ishmael Beah has a way with words, his journey is tragic, but he writes so that he doesn't sound bitter and angry. His words are almost tranquil. Beah sheds light on the controversy in Sierra Leone without sounding morbid. He lets his guard down enough so that the readers can transport themselves and understand the struggles he faced. His words are full of emotions and while reading this book, you cannot help being flooded with the sadness Beah has faced. There is also a sense of happiness and hope in this book.  It shows how truly resilient humans are.<br />The book starts off with a reflection and opening statement on the situation, then, it gets into the journey faced. Beah lived a normal life, where he lived was slightly modern and had a good education. The way he describes his life at the beginning of the books sounds so peaceful, it's almost as if it seems like nothing so terrible could ever go on.  In a blink of an eye on a peaceful day, terror engulfs the town Beah was staying in. The irony is, on this day, Beah described it as being \\"extra peaceful.\\" Beah then describes how he ran from the rebels and tried to stay with his friends. Ishmael and his friends have a brush with danger, rebels had captured their group. By some miracle, the group managed to escape. Although they managed to escape, the group was split apart. Beah now had to not only hide from the rebels, but he now had to try and survive on his own. At this point in the book, the words feel heavy and Beah tries to sound optimistic with his words. The next part of the book he describes trying to find safety with a new group ofyoung boys.<br />Beah then fast forwards through time as a boy soldier, only releasing certain bits and pieces of information because, this topic is very controversial and morbid. He still eloquently describes his predicament enough so that the readers can feel the sorrow boy soldiers felt. Even after he describes his time as a boy soldier and his recovery after being rescued, he still doesn't sound angry and bitter towards the situation. There is still hope in his writing. Eventually, he is saved and described his new life in America. Although this book seems like a simple read, it is full of emotion and is a great narrative on such a devastating situation. This is a book that everyone needs to read; it honestly puts life into perspective and makes the reader feel truly grateful for their life. So much is so eloquently described in just 218 pages.  Big things really do come into small packages.

Yes, I'm that bad.<br />If you are a normal person who can control yourself, you should have fun exploring this manor and the rooms within.  What I especially like about this game, what makes it so different from all the other &#34;hidden object&#34; games, is that most of the objects are out in the open.  And for the most part, they are the same objects every time you go into that particular room.  What changes are the conditions under which you are looking for them, and their placement.  For example, you might be looking for the objects in &#34;night&#34; mode, where the room is totally dark and you just have a very small circle of light that you move about the room in which to find the objects.<br />Also, you are given quests to fulfill throughout the game.  You may have to search a room in a certain mode so many times to unlock another room.<br />You make &#34;friends&#34; by helping other players, and I have &#34;met&#34; people from all over the world.  I think that is one of the reasons why I love the game so much is because there are so many dynamics to it; it's not like other hidden object games where you just go into a room and scour it to find items that are blended into the scene like in a Highlights magazine.<br />There are a couple of things that I'm not so crazy about, but I think they are this way for a reason.  You spend energy when you go into rooms.  It takes a while for your energy to restore and the higher up you get in the game, the longer it takes.  Example: a room might take 40 units of energy.Your energy restores at 1 unit every 3 minutes.  As you get higher in the game, some rooms require 90 units, but you still only restore at 1 every 3 minutes.  So it's very easy to blow through your energy in a matter of ten minutes, then have to wait HOURS for it to restore.<br />Another downside, is almost everything costs &#34;diamonds&#34; if you want to purchase it using game currency.  Earning diamonds is a VERY slow process, and everything costs a crap ton of diamonds.  Probably the #1 complaint is about how slow/hard it is to get diamonds.<br />Can you use real cash to buy diamonds? Sure. Have I? Nope.  I've been playing this game about 4 month, and haven't spent a cent on it, it was even free to download, and I've done fine.  There have been times when I have been super frustrated because I've wished that I've had this or that, but I think if I had unlimited energy, or unlimited diamonds and could just keep going forever, first, I'd NEVER get anything done, and second, there would be no challenge in it for me. 1475972350|2.0|One of the longstanding tropes about the psyche of a murderer is that taking lives gets easier the more you do it. The same, I think, can be said about writing novels. Once you get that first one out of your system and prove that you can actually do it, the more skillful you become.<br /><br />To the author's credit, he has taken that initial bold move and gotten his work published. Although I'm the first to admit that I'm not a fan of the horror genre, I read enough of this material in my capacity as a script consultant for the film industry to be able to assess whether a story's dynamics are plausible and sustainable, its characters are well drawn, and its dialogue sounds natural. The author clearly is a master at painting gory, graphic, and disgusting visuals, and the premise of a serial killer who hates time-wasters is a compelling hook. Unfortunately, the book disappoints on multiple levels, many of which - including a poor job of editing - can hopefully be remedied in future storylines.<br /><br />The first of these is the stilted dialogue. Regardless of the age, gender or occupation of the characters, they all speak in exactly the same \\"voice,\\" never use contractions, and utilize conversations as a device to explain things to the readers rather than genuinely interact with one another. (An easy way for writers to identify this problem is to have friends read their dialogue out loud as if it were a script.) The villain in this tableau is especially repetitious and engages in a long-winded explanation of his motivations that occurs at the halfway point of the novel. Were this a movie, this clich speech would come toward the end of the film and easily allow the heroes enough time to leisurely orchestrate a rescue. Once everything is revealed at the midpoint about the killer's motivations and future plans, it's much too tempting to simply stop reading. Keeping us guessing - and keeping the killer more visible - would have been a better strategy.<br /><br />A lot more research is needed to capture the realities of police investigations and interrogations; as written, none of what is portrayed would pass muster. The pair of lead detectives as well as their counterparts are straight from Central Casting as cop stereotypes and are further encumbered by their inability to figure out any of the clues that are liberally strewn or splashed in their paths. Contrivance abounds at every turn and Max' dark secret about his own past is, alas, not the major reveal the author may have intended. Also problematic are the loopholes insofar as the killer's unexplained ability to effortlessly break into homes, his access to drugs (and copious amounts of duct tape), his flexibility to stalk his future victims, and why his dry cleaner never seems to question all that blood on his clothes. Even throwing in a hot lesbian is not enough to save a storyline that derails fairly early but just doesn't know how to come to a satisfying closure.

1591845556|5.0|The timing of this book suited me well. I had just finished reading [[ASIN:0547884591 The 4-Hour Chef: The Simple Path to Cooking Like a Pro, Learning Anything, and Living the Good Life]] by Timothy Ferriss, another book on rapid skill acquisition, and was in the midst of finishing Josh Kaufman's first book, [[ASIN:1591845572 The Personal MBA: Master the Art of Business]]. This book, The First 20 Hours, was appropriate just-in-time information for me, as opposed to just-in-case information. Another opportunity to look at rapid skill acquisition.<br /><br />Overall, there are two features of this book that caused me to give it a 5* over 4* review:<br /><br />1. Book Layout<br /><br />The first three chapters supply the core of the book's teachings. I read the first three chapters, knew everything I needed to know, and got started brainstorming the prime skill I wanted to focus on. This was instant learning. It took me an afternoon to get started. Many books gloss over important concepts, and make it hard to remember all the useful gold I'm supposed to implement and take to heart. This book went straight to the point.<br /><br />The case studies are six skills learned by the author, following his own advice. How often do you see this? Not very often. He followed his own advice to not only prove it works, but inspire those of us out there who wish to do the same. By having an example to follow, it becomes much easier to visualize ourselves doing this.<br /><br />Kaufman built credibility into his book, and reality. Two important concepts many author's don't present to readers.<br /><br />2. Author's Message<br /><br />Kaufman is not some Nobel Prize winning laureate, or Ph.D. genius. He is an enjoyable author to read, yes, but he is also a regular guy, and a new father. He is tight on time, and he wants to learn new things. His story and teachings resonate with us, because we are him. We want to learn new things too, and we worry about finding the time to do them; especially in Western culture.<br /><br />The purpose of a book is to teach us something we can use, or to help us take a new action. This book has already helped me take new actions, and that is invaluable for a small investment of $12 and three hours of reading.<br /><br />I believe in the author's message, I resonate with him, I root for him, and in turn, I started to root for myself. I can do this too. This book is reality. I've learned that I can truly learn new skills, and it doesn't have to feel like a second job to get good at. That is value.<br /><br />Note: The case-studies aren't essential reading in my opinion, but I found two useful tidbits that may be overlooked by readers:<br /><br />\\"Recent research suggests that, for greatest effect, it's best to sleep within four hours of motor skill practice: even a short nap is better than nothing at all. Any longer, and your brain's ability to consolidate the information it gathered during practice is impaired.\\"<br /><br />\\"Interference is the opposite of consolidation: it's a disruption of the consolidation process. If you practice or use a second, similar skill shortly after practicing a new skill, that practice can interfere with your brain's ability to consolidate the new information.\\"

B004SH40BQ|5.0|This is a runner's review for the KOKKIA i10s paired with JayBird BlueBuds X Sport headphones for the 6th gen ipod nano.  I have been using these for a little over two months now and loving it.  Ever since I purchased my nano two years ago, I longed to wear it as a watch untethered--specifically for jogging.  Together with my bluetooth headphones and my integrated watch band, the KOKKIA helps make that a reality! It is small enough to be virtually unobtrusive, meshing seamlessly with the watch/nano interface.  I also splurged and bought the BlueBud Xs to pair with it, which are pricey, but worth it for the sound quality and fit (see my review of those for more details).<br /><br />JayBird makes a similar bluetooth transmitter to the KOKKIA, but reading the sketchy reviews related to reception, I went with KOKKIA instead.  I am very glad I did.  There is no trick to pairing this with the BlueBuds--you plug it into the interface socket on the nano turn on the headphones, wait for the headphones to tell you they are connected, and off you go.<br /><br />If there is one very minor complaint, it's that on rare occasions, the pairing on startup can take up to a minute for some unknown reason--I have no idea if the delay is due to the transmitter, the headphones, or both.  Most of the time, they connect within 10-15 seconds or faster.  And regardless, once they are connected, they STAY CONNECTED.  I have never had a single instance of a drop, or an issue with interference/bad connection--the transmitter range is beyond 20 feet, and since I wear it on my wrist, this distance is no problem.  The sound is clear and remains so until I turn off the headphones--no matter how much I jump around or flap my arms or whatever.<br /><br />Battery life:  it is certainly true that the transmitter drains the nano battery, and that is to be expected. I usually run for 30-45 minutes at a time, and I can get at least two runs in before I have to recharge the nano, and most of the time I can get three runs in.  Of course, it all depends on how much you use the nano screen as you switch between songs, etc.  I try to keep that to a minimum.  This is perfectly fine for me--those who run longer will need to consider this, but the system should be good for an hour and a half at least.<br /><br />Bottom line: I am finally able to jog with good music and no cords while interfacing with my music device from my wrist--in my opinion, it is the perfect blend for a recreational runner until a HUD becomes feasible (if ever). I am disappointed that Apple has gone away from this design with the next generation--here's hoping my 6th gen lasts for now!get three runs in. B00C061I3K|1.0|Random Access Memories is disheartening to say the least.  Many of its supporters claim that those of us disappointed with it should just accept that Daft Punk is getting a new sound and just get over ourselves, but just because DP has the right to change their sound doesn't mean that we have to buy and enjoy every single album they do.  We have the right to dislike it.<br /><br />This album is unlike anything that Daft Punk has done, and not in a good way.  It's very tedious.  Most albums will have some sort of movement in their tracks, with a range of faster songs and slower songs all mixed together.  All of the songs on this album are mellow. Listening to the whole thing at once is a chore.<br /><br />In any case, I've been listening to the songs for some time now, so that I could give the album a chance.  People call it \\"experimental\\", but you have to realize that most of it is experimental only for Daft Punk -- most of these sounds have been done before, and done to death.  Specifically, by the plethora of DJs you find in the electronic section of a used music store.  This album would not have sold nearly as well had the name on it not been Daft Punk, as it uses many amateur gimmicks, like pretend meaningful lyrics, over dependence on said lyrics with mediocre tunes in the background, \\"creepy\\" sound effects (Touch), and the usage of astronaut recordings (Contact).  I have heard all these things before.<br /><br />I say that, but not all the songs on this are horrible.  Instant Crush is good, and Lose Yourself to Dance is a good song to play at clubs to keep the dancing going.  The other songs, not so much.  Bring Music to Life, while not the worst thing I've heard, is a terrible album opener.  It doesn't have the energy required to do so.  Touch, Fragments of Time, and Game of Love are all pretty annoying.  Giorgio by Moroder had some potential, but it's ruined for replay value by nearly two minutes of talking.  While people rant and rave about Get Lucky, its words are atrocious, and the song is a gimmicky appeal to mainstream audiences.  I honestly didn't believe that Daft Punk was that immature.<br /><br />Upon first listening to the music samples, I figured Motherboard and Contact to be the best songs.  However, this quickly changed.  Motherboard, to me, sounds like a fairly typical soundtrack song just missing a movie.  On further listening, Contact just felt too typical, and also like a shallow attempt to please the older DP fans on an album clearly not for them.<br /><br />I could go on, but I'll just finish up.  Someone said before that if they wanted to listen to normal music, they would.  We don't need another typical, mainstream band.  We need more music that is unique, and isn't going to succumb to the pressure of pop music disease.  After all, Daft Punk got this far by being weird.  Why would they want to be normal?

B005LAII8A|3.0|Looper is a time travel film that is absolutely exciting to watch. Unfortunately, the script plays loose with logic to the point that even normal everyday non-physicists see the glaring loopholes in the story. In one clear example, the future assassin kills a young woman while wounding the jaw of her son. When the younger present-day assassin then kills himself, the woman returns to life since the event technically never happened (i.e., the younger assassin never grew into the older Bruce Willis), yet her younger son's jaw remains injured. Die-hards will try to find other reasons for the still-injured jaw, but in my opinion a good film shouldn't require viewers to struggle to make sense of it. There are many such contradictions throughout the film. Still, the film is fun to watch if you can just ignore the silliness (glaring contradictions).

B00BTHWFBK|4.0|Note: This is an in-depth and lengthy review, so if you don't have the patience for that, please move along. This isn't for you.<br /><br />As true with most bands attempting to put out new material after a lengthy absence, the 3/4 members of Black Sabbath are in an unenviable and inescapable position of having to please an audience made up of very different kinds of fans decades after the height of their popularity. So, 13 was going to be polarizing no matter what. It's 35 years since their last album together. Ozzy isn't going to sound like he did in the '70s (or even '90s), and that reality is going to affect listeners for whom that voice was such a draw on those albums. By the same token, Iommi and Butler aren't the same people or musicians either.<br /><br />To make matter more difficult for them, they chose to move forward without Bill Ward, who the hardcore fan knows was much more than just a drummer. In many ways, Ward was the band's heart, and had a defining influence on their sound. That means that right off the bat, a significant portion of people are going to dismiss this album because of their anger and dissapointment that he was left off it.<br /><br />Additionally, the remaining members' choice to produce with Rick Rubin, who all but destroyed his reputation by over-compressing Metallica's Death Magnetic, was going to turn off another significant faction of the fan-base.<br /><br />On the other side of the divide, there's going to be a large vocal faction who, like a large segment of the Star Wars fanbase, will praise this album regardless of how it turned out just because they're uncritically devoted to Tony, Ozzy and Geezer.<br /><br />As if music wasn't challenging enough to review (due to the fact that it IS so subjective), these factors play a large role in the reviews we're seeing here and elsewhere. And yet, they are legitimate (if divisive) topics of debate.<br /><br />It's helpful for the reader to know a little about the reviewer going in, so in brief, I'm a Black Sabbath fan from the age of 11, and would definitely be considered a hardcore fan in that they're: a) my favorite band, b) I own and actively listen to every album, c) I've read every book on the band and \\"keep up\\" with the individual members. That said, I'm more of a purist in that, while I own the later albums with Dio, Gillen, Hughes, etc., I consider those the work of other bands (e.g., Heaven and Hell for the Dio era, etc.) and not Black Sabbath. So, if you're still with me after that statement, you know that I'm not a casual fan (who really only knows Paranoid and maybe the S/T album or Master of Reality). I love all EIGHT albums, and my favorites tend to change, but they include Sabbath Bloody Sabbath and Never Say Die, as I do NOT subscribe to the idea that Sabbath lost anything after Sabotage. So, yes, I love the fact that they were a heavy, progressive band, not a band that was content to rest on their laurels and repeat themselves.<br /><br />These statements will color my feelings on this album.<br /><br />I do miss Ward on 13. Wilk is a fantastic drummer, and I'm glad it was him and not a traditional \\"metal\\" drummer. Wilk is great, but he isn't as adventurous, jazzy  or idiosyncratic as Ward. But of course he isn't. He's not Bill Ward. And it doesn't help that he's so far back in the mix. But more on the production later. So, yes, a point is taken away due to the fact Iommi/Osbourne/Butler didn't ensure Ward was on here. Forget the tour -- On tour, they could've had two drummers (like the Moody Blues and other bands do) or even a replacement one, if, in fact, Ward can't handle the rigors of the road. But an album is forever, and this was billed as a reunion of the original lineup, so it should be Bill on here, and no one else.<br /><br />With that out of the way, how is the material?<br /><br />IMO, it's excellent to just ok. Iommi, Osbourne and Butler haven't been idle these last 35 years. They've been making music, much of which sounds nothing like '70s-era Black Sabbath. So, attempting to come in after three-plus decades and analyze and understand what it was that made them write and play the way they did is going to be challenging. As a result, you're going to hear modern-era Ozzy and modern-era Iommi on 13. \\"Methademic\\" sounds like something that would've been written for the Heaven and Hell band (or GZR for that matter). \\"Naivete in Black\\" (the Best-Buy exclusive) and \\"Pariah\\" sound like modern-era Ozzy songs. \\"God is Dead?\\" and \\"Dear Father\\" sounds like a combination of the two with the additional attempt to sound like early Sabbath.<br /><br />That latter point has the subject of much praise and lambastment. Songs like \\"End of the Beginning\\" and \\"Zeitgeist\\" sound like old-Sabbath, but too much for some who feel it's a contrived attempt to placate a fanbase, rather than an organic approach to songwriting (in other words, you can't BE Black Sabbath if you're trying to sound like Black Sabbath). In the end, only you, the listener, can determine whether this is a continuation from before, or a plagiarization of it.<br /><br />For ME, I admit that I want what's almost impossible. I want Sabbath to sound like '69-'79 era Sabbath without sounding like they're cribbing from old songs. And you know what? \\"Damaged Soul\\" achieves that near-impossible task! It doesn't remind me of a prior song, but it feels and sounds like something that was left off one of the original albums. If only the rest of the album could've achieved that, but it does come close at times. When it doesn't, it falls flat. \\"End of the Beginning\\" and \\"God is Dead?\\" are just too slow. Sabbath had the reputation of playing slow (given them by the critics), but actually listen to anything past the song \\"Black Sabbath.\\" Even \\"Under the Sun\\" which starts off with a slow, doomy riff, moves into high-gear very quickly. So does \\"Cornucopia,\\" and \\"Into the Void\\" and any other song that seems to be slow, but actually isn't. On 13, however, Rubin seems to go with the critical thinking rather than the reality, and moves the band into glacial territory. This is to the song's detriment. Thankfully, each song changes gear, but far too late. \\"God is Dead?\\" is one stanza too long in the beginning, and it doesn't help that it's a reworking of \\"Black Sabbath.\\" Same with \\"End of the Beginning.\\" Maybe some will like Ozzy singing like grandpa, but I don't find that it works or feels natural and organic. It feels contrived, and I'm tempted to Goldwave the album on a higher speed.<br /><br />When the album does come close to realizing that elusive IT, as the aforementioned \\"Damaged Soul\\" does, it's a thing of beauty, as songs like \\"Loner,\\" \\"Age of Reason\\" and \\"Peace of Mind\\" attest to. Even \\"Life Forever\\" sounds like it could've been left off Vol. 4 (I mean that in the best way). So, we're looking at least 5 out of the 12 songs that have that classic sound to them. WITH one caveat. Don't expect Ozzy to have THAT voice. I actually miss that voice even more than I do Bill Ward. \\"Loner\\" (which bears no small resemblance to \\"Voodoo\\") would've sounded amazing in 1981 (in the alternate world where Ozzy worked out his differences and drug problems and stayed with Sabbath for another few albums). But a big problem is that Ozzy's voice is placed very up-front in the mix. Go back and listen to the first eight albums. Very rarely was that the case (he's up-front on \\"Changes,\\" the exception to the rule). Now that his range and register are diminished, why on earth did Rubin decide to place Ozzy's voice so up-front??<br /><br />Getting back to the songs, even if \\"End of the Beginning\\" and \\"Zeitgeist\\" strike a little too close to earlier material, they're still good songs. In fact, the latter can be considered excellent, though personally I'd have replaced the bongos with something else (thereby eliminating the comparisons to \\"Planet Caravan, and, again, Ozzy's voice is way to forward in the mix). In the end, these songsare the reason I gave the album a four-out-of-five star review. So that brings us to at least 6 out of the 12 songs that are pretty good, and if you have a taste for more modern sounding songs (or \\"God is Dead?\\" which I have ambivalent feelings towards), that number will go up. For me, I love \\"Peace of Mind\\" and would've put it on the album proper, and while not my favorite song, I like the tempo changes in \\"Dear Father\\" so that brings me to enjoying 8 out of 12 songs (which isn't bad.)<br /><br />On the down side, everyone whose angrily complained about the compression is right, and they have every reason to be annoyed. The album is brickwalled, over-compressed and clipped. No, it's not as bad as Death Magnetic, but it's still bad enough that it destroys the dynamic range and breath that these songs need in order to sound truly live and heavy (like the original albums did, which is what Rubin says he was going after). Instead, the production gives the listener ear-fatigue (which no Sabbath album ever had, unless it was remastered that way in the modern day). Do the comparisons yourself. Pop in Sabotage and listen to how those songs live and breathe. Music is meant to have quiet, moderate and loud sections. This fake-loudness that became the trend with hip-hop and MP3s destroys that by mixing the album so that, essentially, all the dials have been turned to 10. Bands like Sabbath are particularly harmed by this because their sound is defined by a kind of musical chiaroscuro effect, light and darkness, soft and heavy, and many of the shades and shadows in between. Think of \\"Sabbath Bloody Sabbath\\" and the way that song alternates back and forth. That kind of sound is ruined by the production of 13. The vinyl is a little better, but only by a margin.<br /><br />The sequencing (the order of songs) doesn't help either. \\"End of the Beginning\\" and \\"God is Dead\\" back-to-back is just bad sequencing, as both songs follow a similar structure and are overly long. Yes, Black Sabbath is known for their long, twisty songs with lots of tempo changes, but they were always varied and didn't FEEL long. These two song have their energy sucked dry. A more astute producer (and that's not a knock to Rubin, but an honest assessment) would've left in Iommi's original (and lengthier) solo in \\"God is Dead?\\" and edited one of the early stanzas instead.<br /><br />Additionally, Iommi/Osbourne/Butler didn't need to crib \\"Black Sabbath's\\" tritone again and again. I understand why they went there. It's what the casual fan expects, and what many who only listen to heavy metal and doom metal want. But it's ultimately un-Sabbathy. Some might recall that Iommi himself stated that \\"not staying in one bag\\" and \\"trying new things\\" was what they were all about, and that's evidenced by their progression from Black Sabbath to Never Say Die. Sadly, many fans (though not all) weren't as open to new ideas and experimentation, as revealed by the unjustly negative reviews afforded Technical Ecstasy and Never Say Die. Since nobody wants to get bad reviews or look bad in the press, Iommi couldn't exactly continue where he left off in the Ozzy-era. Nor could he really, even if he wanted to. He's been playing in a very different style since the '70s.<br /><br />Yet, rightly, few want a Black Sabbath that sounds \\"modern,\\" as evidenced by the general disdain for \\"Psycho Man\\" and \\"Selling My Soul.\\" It's NOT easy to be Black Sabbath, not even for the original members of Black Sabbath. There was a genuine magic back then, an organic driving force. There was a \\"fifth member,\\" as Iommi and Ward often contend. Of course, one has to wonder if there can be fifth member if the fourth is missing?<br /><br />My criticisms aside, I am glad this album exists, but I do feel it could've been better. The production, for one, shouldn't have rendered it so sterile, clinical, cold and compressed. Black Sabbath had some great producers over the years, Rodger Bain, Mike Butcher, even Iommi himself.Rubin's not one of them.  Now credit where it's due: Rubin gave the band direction; it's good that he understands that Sabbath were proto-metal, and a lot jazzier, bluesier and jammier than any heavy band that followed in their wake, and to that end, Rubin deserves praise (though why the band themselves didn't understand that, I don't know; perhaps they did, but the story sounds better that Rubin \\"returned them to their original sound\\"). But why not go the full length then, and retain the natural dynamic range of these songs? Why not have the album sound like the early ones? And why even include songs like \\"Methademic\\" which if we're being honest here, doesn't at all sound like Ozzy-era Sabbath? (I know some people love it, but what I'm saying is based on Rubin's stated goal of doing an album that could sit next to the first few).<br /><br />Of the lyrics, which is something few reviewers ever touch on, they're brilliant as only Geezer Butler can write, but a few seem a bit rushed. \\"Age of Reason\\" is a classic song of social criticism, and in its indictment of the military-industrial complex, organized religion and the masses' tendency towards self-medication and consumerism, it's directly in line with the socially-critical \\"Cornucopia,\\" \\"Killing Yourself to Live,\\" \\"Under the Sun,\\" \\"War Pigs\\" and \\"Wicked World.\\" Osbourne's contributions aren't bad either (Ozzy wrote four lyrics), but are marred by a few clunkers, e.g., \\"I may be dreaming, but whatever\\" (really, Ozzy, \\"whatever\\"?) and \\"Pariah,\\" which really belongs to Ozzy's solo material than Sabbath). Butler tends more towards the existentialism of Vol. 4 this time round, so it's a bit of a downer and not as openly sagelike as Paranoid, Master of Reality and Sabbath Bloody Sabbath are, which may account for the absence of the traditional admonitory tone of Black Sabbath (some of it is there in \\"End of the Beginning,\\" \\"Age of Reason\\" and even \\"Naivete in Black,\\" though that expresses an awkward ambivalence towards the idea of giving direction.) There's also a dearth of the kind of storytelling we see on the debut album or NSD. Overall, it's great to hear Butler's voice again, but on the next album, a little more of Sabbath's strength and spirituality and little less despondency would be great!<br /><br />In the final analysis, get the album, decide for yourself. To me, the original eight albums are perfect. This one could never have lived up to those, but it's still great. Few bands can truly write and execute material that stands alongside the music they made during their creative peak years. So, if you're like me in that regard, and can come to terms with the points I mentioned above, you'll probably enjoy it. Some love this album and find it better than the original eight. That baffles me, but to each his own. For me, I'll re-sequence the songs a bit, place \\"Age of Reason\\" as the album-closer and the excellent \\"Loner\\" as the second song (of either the first or second \\"side\\"), and look for a way to edit the sound so that it's not so damned fatiguing on the ears.<br /><br />Hopefully, on the next album, Iommi/Osbourne and Butler will:<br /><br />a) Get Ward back.<br />b) Find a producer who understands the 70s-era Black Sabbath aesthetics AND the natural dynamics of music (Martin Birch, Tom Allom, Mike Butcher), and that means no compression and putting Ozzy's voice further back in the mix.<br />c) Be more musically adventurous as they once were, and include more instrumentals, variety, and esoteric kinds of piece (e.g., \\"Solitude,\\" \\"Fluff,\\" \\"Spiral Architect,\\" \\"Supertzar,\\" \\"Air Dance,\\" \\"All Moving Parts (Stand Still)\\"), which is going to mean eschewing the idea of appealing to a target audience in favor of being creative and unafraid to experiment, realizing that only they can capture the unique essence of Black Sabbath (as they did on \\"Damaged Soul\\") without needing to borrow the very melodies of early Sabbath.<br /><br />This is a band for whom anything is possible!are the reason I gave the album a four-out-of-five star review. So that brings us to at least 6 out of the 12 songs that are pretty good, and if you have a taste for more modern sounding songs (or \\"God is Dead?\\" which I have ambivalent feelings towards), that number will go up. For me, I love \\"Peace of Mind\\" and would've put it on the album proper, and while not my favorite song, I like the tempo changes in \\"Dear Father\\" so that brings me to enjoying 8 out of 12 songs (which isn't bad.)<br /><br />On the down side, everyone whose angrily complained about the compression is right, and they have every reason to be annoyed. The album is brickwalled, over-compressed and clipped. No, it's not as bad as Death Magnetic, but it's still bad enough that it destroys the dynamic range and breath that these songs need in order to sound truly live and heavy (like the original albums did, which is what Rubin says he was going after). Instead, the production gives the listener ear-fatigue (which no Sabbath album ever had, unless it was remastered that way in the modern day). Do the comparisons yourself. Pop in Sabotage and listen to how those songs live and breathe. Music is meant to have quiet, moderate and loud sections. This fake-loudness that became the trend with hip-hop and MP3s destroys that by mixing the album so that, essentially, all the dials have been turned to 10. Bands like Sabbath are particularly harmed by this because their sound is defined by a kind of musical chiaroscuro effect, light and darkness, soft and heavy, and many of the shades and shadows in between. Think of \\"Sabbath Bloody Sabbath\\" and the way that song alternates back and forth. That kind of sound is ruined by the production of 13. The vinyl is a little better, but only by a margin.<br /><br />The sequencing (the order of songs) doesn't help either. \\"End of the Beginning\\" and \\"God is Dead\\" back-to-back is just bad sequencing, as both songs follow a similar structure and are overly long. Yes, Black Sabbath is known for their long, twisty songs with lots of tempo changes, but they were always varied and didn't FEEL long. These two song have their energy sucked dry. A more astute producer (and that's not a knock to Rubin, but an honest assessment) would've left in Iommi's original (and lengthier) solo in \\"God is Dead?\\" and edited one of the early stanzas instead.<br /><br />Additionally, Iommi/Osbourne/Butler didn't need to crib \\"Black Sabbath's\\" tritone again and again. I understand why they went there. It's what the casual fan expects, and what many who only listen to heavy metal and doom metal want. But it's ultimately un-Sabbathy. Some might recall that Iommi himself stated that \\"not staying in one bag\\" and \\"trying new things\\" was what they were all about, and that's evidenced by their progression from Black Sabbath to Never Say Die. Sadly, many fans (though not all) weren't as open to new ideas and experimentation, as revealed by the unjustly negative reviews afforded Technical Ecstasy and Never Say Die. Since nobody wants to get bad reviews or look bad in the press, Iommi couldn't exactly continue where he left off in the Ozzy-era. Nor could he really, even if he wanted to. He's been playing in a very different style since the '70s.<br /><br />Yet, rightly, few want a Black Sabbath that sounds \\"modern,\\" as evidenced by the general disdain for \\"Psycho Man\\" and \\"Selling My Soul.\\" It's NOT easy to be Black Sabbath, not even for the original members of Black Sabbath. There was a genuine magic back then, an organic driving force. There was a \\"fifth member,\\" as Iommi and Ward often contend. Of course, one has to wonder if there can be fifth member if the fourth is missing?<br /><br />My criticisms aside, I am glad this album exists, but I do feel it could've been better. The production, for one, shouldn't have rendered it so sterile, clinical, cold and compressed. Black Sabbath had some great producers over the years, Rodger Bain, Mike Butcher, even Iommi himself.Rubin's not one of them.  Now credit where it's due: Rubin gave the band direction; it's good that he understands that Sabbath were proto-metal, and a lot jazzier, bluesier and jammier than any heavy band that followed in their wake, and to that end, Rubin deserves praise (though why the band themselves didn't understand that, I don't know; perhaps they did, but the story sounds better that Rubin \\"returned them to their original sound\\"). But why not go the full length then, and retain the natural dynamic range of these songs? Why not have the album sound like the early ones? And why even include songs like \\"Methademic\\" which if we're being honest here, doesn't at all sound like Ozzy-era Sabbath? (I know some people love it, but what I'm saying is based on Rubin's stated goal of doing an album that could sit next to the first few).<br /><br />Of the lyrics, which is something few reviewers ever touch on, they're brilliant as only Geezer Butler can write, but a few seem a bit rushed. \\"Age of Reason\\" is a classic song of social criticism, and in its indictment of the military-industrial complex, organized religion and the masses' tendency towards self-medication and consumerism, it's directly in line with the socially-critical \\"Cornucopia,\\" \\"Killing Yourself to Live,\\" \\"Under the Sun,\\" \\"War Pigs\\" and \\"Wicked World.\\" Osbourne's contributions aren't bad either (Ozzy wrote four lyrics), but are marred by a few clunkers, e.g., \\"I may be dreaming, but whatever\\" (really, Ozzy, \\"whatever\\"?) and \\"Pariah,\\" which really belongs to Ozzy's solo material than Sabbath). Butler tends more towards the existentialism of Vol. 4 this time round, so it's a bit of a downer and not as openly sagelike as Paranoid, Master of Reality and Sabbath Bloody Sabbath are, which may account for the absence of the traditional admonitory tone of Black Sabbath (some of it is there in \\"End of the Beginning,\\" \\"Age of Reason\\" and even \\"Naivete in Black,\\" though that expresses an awkward ambivalence towardsthe idea of giving direction.) There's also a dearth of the kind of storytelling we see on the debut album or NSD. Overall, it's great to hear Butler's voice again, but on the next album, a little more of Sabbath's strength and spirituality and little less despondency would be great!<br /><br />In the final analysis, get the album, decide for yourself. To me, the original eight albums are perfect. This one could never have lived up to those, but it's still great. Few bands can truly write and execute material that stands alongside the music they made during their creative peak years. So, if you're like me in that regard, and can come to terms with the points I mentioned above, you'll probably enjoy it. Some love this album and find it better than the original eight. That baffles me, but to each his own. For me, I'll re-sequence the songs a bit, place \\"Age of Reason\\" as the album-closer and the excellent \\"Loner\\" as the second song (of either the first or second \\"side\\"), and look for a way to edit the sound so that it's not so damned fatiguing on the ears.<br /><br />Hopefully, on the next album, Iommi/Osbourne and Butler will:<br /><br />a) Get Ward back.<br />b) Find a producer who understands the 70s-era Black Sabbath aesthetics AND the natural dynamics of music (Martin Birch, Tom Allom, Mike Butcher), and that means no compression and putting Ozzy's voice further back in the mix.<br />c) Be more musically adventurous as they once were, and include more instrumentals, variety, and esoteric kinds of piece (e.g., \\"Solitude,\\" \\"Fluff,\\" \\"Spiral Architect,\\" \\"Supertzar,\\" \\"Air Dance,\\" \\"All Moving Parts (Stand Still)\\"), which is going to mean eschewing the idea of appealing to a target audience in favor of being creative and unafraid to experiment, realizing that only they can capture the unique essence of Black Sabbath (as they did on \\"Damaged Soul\\") without needing to borrow the very melodies of early Sabbath.<br /><br />This is a band for whom anything is possible!

B00AQB2A3I|5.0|Steven Wilson is arguably a modern genius of the prog community along with Mikael Akerfeldt, yet when they both came together for the Storm Corrosion project it had little impact on me. Wilson however as a solo artist has become intensely passionate about his music, and his solo albums are incredible masterpieces, especially 'Grace For Drowning' that floored me to the point where I had to obtain the deluxe version. Having to follow up such a brilliant album is not an easy thing but somehow Wilson has done so with a flourish that has heralded in the 2013 year in admirable style. Alan Parsons was on board to engineer this album so one would have to expect a high quality sound and it doesn't disappoint.<br /><br />The album cover is like a rash all over the net with the astonished moon looming full and iconic in the darkness. The artwork is simple but embeds itself into the conscious easily and thus does its job to gain attention. The rest of the artwork in the Deluxe Version booklet consists of line drawings, some colourful paintings of the dishevelled looking kid in dense locations, a miserable man looking into a beer glass, later drinking a shot, some disturbing scratchy drawings, darkened stairwells and window frames, creepy faces staring out, images of a house, a tree and the scrawny watchmaker at work as his wife looks on, frames from the 'Raven' video clip with snow falling down in the forest, and the shivering old man pursuing the elusive bird. The booklet is extensive and arty as one might expect, and ends with an amusing drawing of the band playing looking like thin men with Wilson headbanging away. The artwork on the CD is the Raven looking mystical and enigmatic, and of course if you did get the Deluxe package you also have a 7 song demo to indulge in and the album in 5.1 sound on a blu ray and a DVD thrown in with all the clips and interviews.<br /><br />'The Raven That Refused To Sing' has been promoted with film clips hovering about on the internet way before its release date and the film clip images that accompanies the title track are extraordinary. Wilson has reinvented himself again on this album, discarding the darkness of 'Grace For Drowning', and embracing a sound more akin to Porcupine Tree, oddly enough. The title track is masterful, and as it was the first track I heard initially I will start here. It is laced with beautiful keyboards and a pretty melody masking the downbeat lyrics that focus on the man's dead sister, that haunts the storyteller like the raven, and he misses her terribly and dreams of her to return to him; 'Just because I'm weak, You can steal my dreams, You can reach inside my head, And you can put your song there instead.'<br /><br />Lyrically the poetry in the song has a melancholy edge. The images on the clip of an old man in a forest encountering a raven and then pursuing it finally capturing it and then dying, have a profound symbolic resonance. According to Wilson, the songs have classic Gothic ideas interspersed with suggested dread, regret, loss and the fear of mortality, or impending death, thus the omen of the Raven. It is these ideas that create a very unique atmosphere on the album. The raven essentially becomes, in the mind of the old man, a reincarnation of the old man's dead sister, and in his own delusion he believes if he can capture the raven and hear him sing he can recapture the life of his dead sister. The music is stripped back at times to a piano reverberating in the stillness. Wilson knows how to build on musical ideas and surpasses himself with such tracks, the mesmirising and haunting beauty is superb.<br /><br />The album opens with 'Luminol' with a delightful pulsating bassline and reverb wah wah guitar splashes. Musically the album is faultless and the flute enhances the quality. There are some wonderful Yes-like multilayered harmonies for a moment and then the extended musical break dominates, with an odd time sig, an intense spectrum of bass, pounding percussion, floating flute, and Mellotron sounds. The electric piano runs have a 70s vibe especially when it builds with a shimmering soundscape, and utilising distortion devices and a ring modulator to good effect. At 4:35 it settles into a minimalist rhythm guitar and Wilson's vocals, in his reflective mood, with references to pop culture, 'the songs he learned from scratched LPs, stops in mid flow to sip his tea.' The lyrics centre on a protagonist who has died even in death continues to discover answers through reminiscing on the past or reflecting on a life that has faded; 'He strums the chords with less than grace, Each passing year etched on his face', is a reflection on how one might feel as we are 'born into a struggle, To come so far but end up returning to dust.' This ghost is a metaphor of fear and our obsession with mortality, according to Wilson in his online interviews.<br /><br />Wilson doesn't labour on grim themes or death however throughout, and this album has more rays of hope than the shadows of despair found on previous releases, and leaves one with a profound sense of fulfilment. The music is uplifting and energetic, infused with passion throughout and progressive ideas using all the musicians at Wilson's disposal. The flute playing of Theo Travis is exquisite, but I am a real fan of that grinding organ by Adam Holzman, and the way the guitar interplays creating those endearing melodies. 'Luminol' is a masterpiece of the album and a promise of things to come.<br /><br />Next is 'Drive Home' that opens with a sweet Neo-Classical melody that sounds partially like 'Castle In The Clouds' from 'Les Mesirables'. When Wilson comes in on vocals, the signature locks into a steady measured pace. All is held back like the old days of Porcupine Tree or the 'Deadwing' era. There is a remarkable beauty that emanates from the Mellotrons that sound like violins. The chorus is Wilson at his most melancholy with thought provoking lyrics; 'You need to clear away all the jetsam in your brain, And face the truth, Well love can make amends, While the darkness always ends, You're still alone so drive home.' The instrumental break is stripped back to a nice fingerpicking guitar motif, like Steve Howe, and Travis's achingly beautiful flute is layered over. The lead guitar break of Guthrie Govan that follows is incredible, soaring emotionally and adds so much depth to the overall atmosphere.<br /><br />'The Holy Drinker' is a glorious throwback to the eclectic prog of vintage King Crimson meets Van der Graaf Generator, with elements of jazz fusion and mind blowing dissonant saxophone blasts. It opens with the wavering keyboard sound heard on Van der Graaf Generator's 'A Plague Of Lighthouse Keepers', then breaks into a cacophony of sound like a jazz shop exploded. Theo Travis is master at the sax helm and Wilson is the commander as he constructs this disharmony of musical instruments. I adored this on first listen and it soon became the quintessential track of the album for me. After this outlandish intro, the song finds some semblance of structure and Wilson sings some odd lyrics that I don't want to think about too deeply; 'With shaking hands and blackened heart, The glass he pours, this time it's also the last, In rapt communion with himself, The Holy Drinker is going straight in to hell.' On cue the song breaks into extended soloing with some wonderful organ and chirping flute taking centre stage. I love how the organ has that Keith Emerson 'Tarkus' sound at 6:25, but a special mention goes to the sporadic drumming of Marco Minnemann and Bass of Nick Beggs that are always on target and played to perfection. The song settles into Wilson's echoing gentle voice at about 8 minutes in, but it feels ominous as though the jazz fusion will break out at any moment. Then a grinding Van der Graaf Generator organ sound growls viciously with a downbeat tone, joined by odd rhythmic guitars. This feels like the coda of VDGG's 'White Hammer' andit is ferociously off kilter enough to jar the senses to their most awakened state. AlI in all a furious blast of masterful music and one to seek out for those interested in checking out the best on the album.<br /><br />Thus far the album is astonishing, nothing less than brilliant prog, so I was looking forward to the next half. 'The Pin Drop' is the shortest song at 5.03, and has Wilson on his highest register tone singing; 'Carried away by the river that passes through bulrushes on to the sea, Dragged by the current to rest on the stakes of the breakwater shaded by trees, Beginnings and endings, love intersecting a rift that will break us apart.' The return of the sax is so welcome, and Travis lifts off with massive runs and haunting squeals of jazz ecstasy. The song moves into a Twilight Zone like atmosphere melodically, and feels again like vintage Porcupine Tree. The layered harmonies are exceptional and create a wall of sound, and all is augmented by the accomplished lead guitar solo of Govan. All this in 5 minutes, simply incredible work from Mr Wilson.<br /><br />'The Watchmaker' is another of the album's epics, and showcases Wilson's poetry in the lyrics; 'The watchmaker buries something deep within his thoughts, A shadow on the staircase of someone from before, This thing is broken now and cannot be repaired, Fifty years of compromise and aging bodies shared, Eliza dear, you know there's something I should say, I never really loved you but I'll miss you anyway.' The music is appropriately like a music box chiming, very Gabriel-era Genesis in fact, and is enhanced by dreamy flute embellishments. There is a glorious lead guitar solo, perhaps the best on the album with Govan taking on speedy licks effortlessly and the squeaky sax joins in and it suddenly reminds me of Pink Floyd. The song takes on a new format then with piano runs and Wilson's voice emanating thoughts of the Watchmaker who reminisces on a dark deed involving the murder of his wife who has returned from the grave; 'But for you I had to wait, Until one day it was too late.' The music and harmonies become more romantically intertwined utilising old school 'do do do's' and then finally it breaks out into an odd time sig and some glistening piano sparkles, a booming bass solo reminding me of Rush's Geddy Lee. Finally the next phase of the music becomes dissonant with weird off tones in a 7/8 meter, being used where they should not, creating a disquieteing effect. This is a complex piece of music and perhaps the darkest track on the whole album, more like the 'Grace For Drowning' themes than others on offer.<br /><br />It ends of course with the beauty of 'The Raven That Refused To Sing' and we are left with an astonishing album of dark haunting power as only Wilson knows how to create. This is certainly a different creature than 'Grace For Drowning' and did not impact me like that masterpiece, and yet this latest release is mesmerising on every listen. It is an album to listen to with unwavering focus, as is all of Wilson's work; music designed for headphones. It is hard to rate this album as everything is so well placed and perfect; Wilson throws in so many ideas that it is impossible not to enjoy this if your ears are attuned to experimental progressive ideas. It did not measure up to 'Grace For Drowning' for me, but still is a masterful album presented in a compact form of less than an hour. If you want more, the Deluxe version is ample enough though did not add that much musically, but more artistically. The experience is sheer joy when an album comes out that embraces all that is great about prog. The sax, the organ, the lead guitars, the rhythms; all are played to perfection. Wilson's voice is faultless and his ideas are poetically conveyed to precision.<br /><br />Perhaps it is too perfect and too calculating for some listeners and I can understand how this can be off putting, and why it has received mixed critical reactions. However, Wilson is nothing short of passionate about his music and every note is placed to generate a congenial effect to enhance the overall experience. This is a series of stories, as the album titles states, and each story has its own atmosphere and style though there is a consistency in the thematic juxtaposition of music and vocals. It is a far superior album to some of the earlier Porcupine Tree albums and indeed Wilson's debut solo. I would rank it easily among his greatest triumphs, and certainly it is going to be one of the albums of the year. An album this bold and inventive deserves full recommendation and thus far it is the best release in 2013, so 5 shining stars to a modern musical genius that continues to produce prog at its highest caliber.it is ferociously off kilter enough to jar the senses to their most awakened state. AlI in all a furious blast of masterful music and one to seek out for those interested in checking out the best on the album.<br /><br />Thus far the album is astonishing, nothing less than brilliant prog, so I was looking forward to the next half. 'The Pin Drop' is the shortest song at 5.03, and has Wilson on his highest register tone singing; 'Carried away by the river that passes through bulrushes on to the sea, Dragged by the current to rest on the stakes of the breakwater shaded by trees, Beginnings and endings, love intersecting a rift that will break us apart.' The return of the sax is so welcome, and Travis lifts off with massive runs and haunting squeals of jazz ecstasy. The song moves into a Twilight Zone like atmosphere melodically, and feels again like vintage Porcupine Tree. The layered harmonies are exceptional and create a wall of sound, and all is augmented by the accomplished lead guitar solo of Govan. All this in 5 minutes, simply incredible work from Mr Wilson.<br /><br />'The Watchmaker' is another of the album's epics, and showcases Wilson's poetry in the lyrics; 'The watchmaker buries something deep within his thoughts, A shadow on the staircase of someone from before, This thing is broken now and cannot be repaired, Fifty years of compromise and aging bodies shared, Eliza dear, you know there's something I should say, I never really loved you but I'll miss you anyway.' The music is appropriately like a music box chiming, very Gabriel-era Genesis in fact, and is enhanced by dreamy flute embellishments. There is a glorious lead guitar solo, perhaps the best on the album with Govan taking on speedy licks effortlessly and the squeaky sax joins in and it suddenly reminds me of Pink Floyd. The song takes on a new format then with piano runs and Wilson's voice emanating thoughts of the Watchmaker who reminisces on a dark deed involving the murder of his wife who has returned from the grave; 'But for you I had to wait, Until one day it was too late.' The music and harmonies become more romantically intertwined utilising old school 'do do do's' and then finally it breaks out into an odd time sig and some glistening piano sparkles, a booming bass solo reminding me of Rush's Geddy Lee. Finally the next phase of the music becomes dissonant with weird off tones in a 7/8 meter, being used where they should not, creating a disquieteing effect. This is a complex piece of music and perhaps the darkest track on the whole album, more like the 'Grace For Drowning' themes than others on offer.<br /><br />It ends of course with the beauty of 'The Raven That Refused To Sing' and we are left with an astonishing album of dark haunting power as only Wilson knows how to create. This is certainly a different creature than 'Grace For Drowning' and did not impact me like that masterpiece, and yet this latest release is mesmerising on every listen. It is an album to listen to with unwavering focus, as is all of Wilson's work; music designed for headphones. It is hard to rate this album as everything is so well placed and perfect; Wilson throws in so many ideas that it is impossible not to enjoy this if your ears are attuned to experimental progressive ideas. It did not measure up to 'Grace For Drowning' for me, but still is a masterful album presented in a compact form of less than an hour. If you want more, the Deluxe version is ample enough though did not add that much musically, but more artistically. The experience is sheer joy when an album comes out that embraces all that is great about prog. The sax, the organ, the lead guitars, the rhythms; all are played to perfection. Wilson's voice is faultless and his ideas are poetically conveyed to precision.<br /><br />Perhaps it is too perfect and too calculating for some listeners and I can understand how this can be off putting, and why it has received mixed critical reactions. However, Wilson is nothing short of passionate about his music and every note is placed to generate a congenial effect to enhance the overall experience. This is a series of stories, as the album titles states, and each story has its own atmosphere and style though there is a consistency in the thematic juxtaposition of music and vocals. It is a far superior album to some of the earlier Porcupine Tree albums and indeed Wilson's debut solo. I would rank it easily among his greatest triumphs, and certainly it is going to be one of the albums of the year. An album this bold and inventive deserves full recommendation and thus far it is the best release in 2013, so 5 shining stars to a modern musical genius that continues to produce prog at its highest caliber.
